import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1):
        """
        Vision Transformer (ViT) model.

        :param image_size: The size of the input image (assumed to be square).
        :param patch_size: The size of each patch (assumed to be square).
        :param num_classes: The number of output classes.
        :param dim: The dimensionality of the embedding space.
        :param depth: The number of transformer layers.
        :param heads: The number of attention heads.
        :param mlp_dim: The dimensionality of the MLP (Multi-Layer Perceptron) in the transformer.
        :param channels: The number of channels in the input image (default is 3 for RGB).
        :param dropout: Dropout rate applied in the MLP.
        :param emb_dropout: Dropout rate applied to the embedded patches.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        assert image_size % patch_size == 0, "Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size."
        num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2
        patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2
        
        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim))
        self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim)
        self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim))
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout)
        
        self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder(
            nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout),
            num_layers=depth
        )
        
        self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity()
        self.mlp_head = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(dim, mlp_dim),
            nn.GELU(),
            nn.Dropout(dropout),
            nn.Linear(mlp_dim, num_classes)
        )
    
    def forward(self, img):
        """
        Forward pass of the Vision Transformer.

        :param img: The input image tensor, shape (batch_size, channels, image_size, image_size).
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes).
        """
        p = self.patch_size
        
        x = img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p).reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1])
        x = self.patch_to_embedding(x)
        
        cls_tokens = self.cls_token.expand(img.shape[0], -1, -1)
        x = torch.cat((cls_tokens, x), dim=1)
        x += self.pos_embedding
        x = self.dropout(x)
        
        x = self.transformer(x)
        
        x = self.to_cls_token(x[:, 0])
        return self.mlp_head(x)

# Test code
image_size = 224
patch_size = 16
num_classes = 10
dim = 512
depth = 6
heads = 8
mlp_dim = 2048
channels = 3
dropout = 0.0
emb_dropout = 0.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(2, channels, image_size, image_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels, dropout, emb_dropout]

We need to implement a custom CUDA kernel for the forward pass of a small Vision Transformer (ViT) model. The goal is to produce numerically equivalent output and be faster than the reference PyTorch implementation. Patch extraction: `img.unfold(2, p, p).unfold(3, p, p).reshape(img.shape[0], -1, p*p*img.shape[1])` - This extracts 16x16 patches from the 224x224 image - Number of patches = (224/16)^2 = 14^2 = 196 - Each patch has 3*16*16 = 768 values - Output shape: (batch, 196, 768) 2. Patch embedding: `self.patch_to_embedding(x)` - a Linear layer from 768 to 512 - Output shape: (batch, 196, 512) 3. Concatenate CLS token: `cls_tokens = self.cls_token.expand(img.shape[0], -1, -1)` - Shape: (batch, 1, 512) - `torch.cat((cls_tokens, x), dim=1)` -> (batch, 197, 512) 4. Add positional embedding: `x += self.pos_embedding` (learned positional embeddings) - Shape: (1, 197, 512) broadcasted to (batch, 197, 512) 5. Dropout (rate 0.0, so identity) 6. Transformer encoder: 6 layers of standard PyTorch TransformerEncoderLayer - Each layer: MultiHeadAttention + AddNorm + FeedForward + AddNorm - d_model=512, nhead=8, dim_feedforward=2048 7. Extract CLS token: `x[:, 0]` -> (batch, 512) 8. MLP head: - Linear(512, 2048) - GELU - Dropout(0.0) - Linear(2048, 10) The whole model is quite large to replace with a single CUDA kernel, but we can try to fuse as much as possible and optimize the transformer layers. Let me write the code. **Preprocess CUDA kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* img, // [B, C, H, W] const float* patch_weight, // [dim, patch_dim] const float* patch_bias, // [dim] const float* cls_token, // [1, 1, dim] const float* pos_embedding,// [1, seq, dim] float* out, // [B, seq, dim] int B, int C, int H, int W, int patch_size, int num_patches, int patch_dim, int dim, float dropout_p, int seed) { int b = blockIdx.x; int token = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // token 0 is cls_token, tokens 1..num_patches are patches if (token == 0) { // Copy cls_token for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float val = cls_token[d]; out[b * num_patches * dim + d] = val; } return; } int patch_idx = token - 1; int py = patch_idx / (W / patch_size); int px = patch_idx % (W / patch_size); // Compute linear projection for this patch // Each thread handles some output dimensions for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = patch_bias[d]; const float* w_row = patch_weight + d * patch_dim; int idx = 0; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { for (int dy = 0; dy < patch_size; dy++) { for (int dx = 0; dx < patch_size; dx++) { int iy = py * patch_size + dy; int ix = px * patch_size + dx; float pixel = img[b * C * H * W + c * H * W + iy * W + ix]; sum += pixel * w_row[idx]; idx++; } } } // Add pos_embedding sum += pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; // Apply dropout if needed if (dropout_p > 0.0f) { // Simple LCG RNG int rng_state = seed + b * num_patches * dim + token * dim + d; rng_state = (rng_state * 1103515245 + 12345) & 0x7fffffff; float rand = (float)rng_state / (float)0x7fffffff; if (rand < dropout_p) { sum = 0.0f; } else { sum /= (1.0f - dropout_p); } } out[b * num_patches * dim + d] = sum; } } ``` Wait, for token indexing: `out` should be [B, seq, dim] where seq = num_patches + 1. And cls_token goes at position 0, followed by the image patch embeddings. Let me fix the indexing. Actually, writing a kernel with each thread computing one output dimension for one token is fine. For dim=512, I'd use 256 or 512 threads per block. For the patch pixels: each thread loads them from global memory. The loads are all coalesced within a warp if the pixels are accessed in a specific order... but actually, different threads in the same warp (handling different output dimensions) will all read the same pixels. This means the pixels will be read repeatedly, but they'll be cached in L1 since all threads in the warp read the same pixels. This is actually pretty efficient. For a Vision Transformer with batch=2 on H100: - The model has ~18M parameters - Each forward pass reads all parameters once and writes all activations - For 6 layers, the compute is ~8B FLOPs - At 67 TFLOPS FP32, pure compute is ~120µs - But memory bandwidth for reading weights once: 18M * 4 bytes = 72MB. At 3.35 TB/s = 94µs. So total theoretical time is around 100-200µs. Kernel launch overhead of 50 * 5µs = 250µs would dominate! Ah, this is the key insight. For small batch sizes on fast GPUs, kernel launch overhead dominates the execution time in standard PyTorch. To get speedup, I need to FUSE operations into fewer kernels. With `nn.TransformerEncoderLayer`, even though it calls a few functions, internally each function (like LayerNorm) might launch multiple kernels. And if PyTorch uses the math backend for attention instead of fused attention, that's many more kernels. On H100, `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` should use a fused kernel, so using it explicitly is important. **My revised strategy:** 1. For LayerNorm, use PyTorch's F.layer_norm... but wait, can I fuse LayerNorm + following Linear? No. Actually, for LayerNorm followed by Linear, it might be possible to write a fused kernel. Wait, for the transformer with norm_first=False: ``` src2 = self.norm1(src) src2 = self_attn(src2) src = src + src2 src2 = self.norm2(src) src2 = self.linear2(self.activation(self.linear1(src2))) src = src + src2 ``` There's no add+layernorm fusion opportunity in standard post-LN. The residual is added after attention, then the next layer norm is applied to that sum. But for the attention output, it's just `src + attn_out`, which is a simple add. For the FFN output, it's `src + ffn_out`, which is also a simple add. The most beneficial fusions are the ones that reduce memory bandwidth for the most expensive operations. In this model: - Matmuls use cuBLAS and are compute-bound or reasonably efficient - Elementwise ops (LayerNorm, GELU, adds, dropout) are bandwidth-bound and have kernel launch overhead Let me count the minimum number of kernels I need if I fully fuse what I can: For each layer: 1. **LayerNorm kernel**: read src, write src_norm 2. **QKV matmul**: src_norm @ W_qkv (cuBLAS) → qkv 3. **Attention kernel**: reshape + sdpa + out_proj @ W_out (or call PyTorch's efficient attention) 4. **Add kernel**: src + attn_out → src 5. **LayerNorm kernel**: read src, write src_norm 6. **FFN1 matmul**: src_norm @ W1 (cuBLAS) → hidden 7. **GELU + dropout kernel**: read hidden, write hidden_act 8. **FFN2 matmul**: hidden_act @ W2 (cuBLAS) → ffn_out 9. **Add kernel**: src + ffn_out → src Wait, actually `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` already includes the matmuls for attention, but not the QKV projection or output projection. So step 3 would be multiple internal kernels if done naively. Actually, looking at manual attention implementations, `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` only replaces the `softmax(Q@K^T / sqrt(d)) @ V` part. The QKV projections and output projections are separate matmuls. So for attention: 3a. QKV projection matmul 3b. Reshape for heads 3c. `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` 3d. Reshape back 3e. Output projection matmul Steps 3b, 3c, 3d happen entirely within `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` if we use the flash attention approach, but the QKV and output projections are still separate. Actually, on H100 with fp16/bf16, there might be kernels that fuse QKV projection + attention + output projection. Let me try to write a kernel that fuses the entire MLP head: ```cuda __global__ void mlp_head_kernel( const float* x, // [B, dim] const float* w1, // [mlp_dim, dim] const float* b1, // [mlp_dim] const float* w2, // [num_classes, mlp_dim] const float* b2, // [num_classes] float* out, // [B, num_classes] int B, int dim, int mlp_dim, int num_classes) { int b = blockIdx.x; // Load input into shared memory or registers float input[512]; // fixed for dim=512 for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { input[d] = x[b * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute first linear layer // Each thread handles some mlp_dim outputs for (int m = threadIdx.x; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += input[d] * w1[m * dim + d]; } // GELU sum = gelu(sum); // Second linear layer needs to be computed differently... } } ``` For the second linear layer, I'd need to store the intermediate in shared memory. mlp_dim=2048, which at float32 is 8KB, easily fitting in shared memory. So the MLP head kernel can be fully fused: Linear1 → GELU → Linear2 → output. Similarly, for each FFN in the transformer: Linear1 → GELU → Dropout → Linear2. For batch=2, seq=197, each token's FFN can be computed independently. For one token: input [512] → hidden [2048] → output [512]. I can write a kernel where each block handles one token, and the block computes the entire FFN for that token using shared memory. 512 input * 4 bytes = 2KB. 2048 hidden * 4 bytes = 8KB. 512 output * 4 bytes = 2KB. Total shared memory needed per block: ~12KB for activations + weight tiles. This is very manageable! For QKV projection: input [512] → output [1536] (Q+K+V). This is a dot product of 1536 outputs, each being a 512-element dot product. For one block per token: 1536 threads or fewer. 1536 * 512 = 786K multiplies per token. For 394 tokens, total 310M multiplies. At H100's ~30 TFLOPS FP32 for simple loops (without Tensor Cores), this is 310M / 30T = ~10µs. With shared memory and proper tiling, maybe 5µs. This is actually very fast! For attention: Q[64], K[64], V[64] for each of 8 heads. The attention scores are 197x197 per head. Computing this manually per token: For one token, the attention output is a weighted sum of all 197 value vectors. Each token can compute its own output in parallel. For one head, one token needs to: 1. Compute dot products with all 197 keys: 197 * 64 = ~12K ops 2. Softmax over 197 3. Weighted sum of 197 values: 197 * 64 = ~12K ops Total: ~24K ops per head per token. For 8 heads: ~192K ops per token. For 394 tokens: ~75M ops. At 30 TFLOPS: ~2.5µs. So attention done manually in CUDA could be ~2.5µs! For output projection: [512] → [512], ~262K ops per token, 394 tokens = ~103M ops, ~3.4µs. So one full transformer layer: - LN: ~1µs - QKV: ~10µs - Attention: ~2.5µs - Out proj: ~3.4µs - Add: ~0.1µs - LN: ~1µs - FFN1: ~10µs (512→2048) - GELU+dropout: ~0.5µs - FFN2: ~10µs (2048→512) - Add: ~0.1µs Total per layer: ~38.6µs. For 6 layers: ~232µs. And this is all without Tensor Cores, using simple CUDA! Plus, we can fuse more operations to reduce memory traffic. Actually, these estimates assume peak throughput for simple kernels, which might not be realistic. But they're in the right ballpark. For comparison, what would PyTorch do? For step 2, let me write out the exact manual transformer: ```python def forward(self, img): # Step 1: Custom CUDA preprocessing x = preprocess_cuda(img, self.patch_to_embedding.weight, self.patch_to_embedding.bias, self.cls_token, self.pos_embedding, self.training, self.dropout.p) # Step 2: Transformer for layer in self.transformer.layers: # Norm 1 x2 = F.layer_norm(x, layer.norm1.normalized_shape, layer.norm1.weight, layer.norm1.bias, layer.norm1.eps) # Self attention # Extract QKV projection qkv = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) # Reshape for attention B, S, _ = q.shape q = q.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) # Efficient attention attn_out = F.scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v) attn_out = attn_out.transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, S, self.dim) # Output projection x2 = F.linear(attn_out, layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) x = x + layer.dropout1(x2) # Norm 2 x2 = F.layer_norm(x, layer.norm2.normalized_shape, layer.norm2.weight, layer.norm2.bias, layer.norm2.eps) # FFN x2 = F.linear(x2, layer.linear1.weight, layer.linear1.bias) x2 = self._activation(x2) x2 = layer.linear2(layer.dropout(x2)) x = x + layer.dropout2(x2) x = x[:, 0] return self.mlp_head(x) ``` Wait, `nn.TransformerEncoder` also adds a final `norm` if `norm is not None`. Let me optimize the image loading: - For a given (batch, patch_y, patch_x), the image pixels are at img[batch, c, patch_y*16 + dy, patch_x*16 + dx] - To load all 768 pixels efficiently, the threads can load them into shared memory - 768 floats = 3072 bytes, well within shared memory limits Then all threads read from shared memory to compute their dot products. This should be very efficient! Here's the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ cls_token, const float* __restrict__ pos_embedding, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int H, int W, int patch_size, int num_patches, int patch_dim, int dim, int dropout_p, unsigned int seed) { extern __shared__ float s_patch[]; int b = blockIdx.x; int token = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // Token 0 is CLS token if (token == 0) { for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float val = cls_token[d] + pos_embedding[d]; out[(b * num_patches + 0) * dim + d] = val; } return; } int patch_idx = token - 1; int py = patch_idx / (W / patch_size); int px = patch_idx % (W / patch_size); // Load patch pixels into shared memory // Total pixels: C * patch_size * patch_size int total_pixels = patch_dim; for (int i = tid; i < total_pixels; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (patch_size * patch_size); int rem = i % (patch_size * patch_size); int dy = rem / patch_size; int dx = rem % patch_size; int iy = py * patch_size + dy; int ix = px * patch_size + dx; s_patch[i] = img[((b * C + c) * H + iy) * W + ix]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute linear projection // Each thread computes some output dimensions for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[d]; const float* w_row = weight + d * patch_dim; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) { sum += s_patch[i] * w_row[i]; } // Add positional embedding sum += pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; // Dropout if (dropout_p > 0) { unsigned int rng = seed + b * num_patches * dim + token * dim + d; rng = (rng * 1103515245 + 12345); float rand_val = (float)(rng & 0x7fffffff) / (float)0x7fffffff; if (rand_val < dropout_p) { sum = 0.0f; } else { sum /= (1.0f - dropout_p); } } out[(b * num_patches + token) * dim + d] = sum; } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the output indexing. If num_patches = 196, then seq = 197, and cls_token is at index 0. But looking at the original model: ```python x = torch.cat((cls_tokens, x), dim=1) x += self.pos_embedding ``` `self.pos_embedding` is [1, num_patches + 1, dim]. So pos_embedding covers all seq tokens. For token 0 (CLS), the output should be: `cls_token[0, 0, d] + pos_embedding[0, 0, d]` For token 1..196: `linear(patch) + pos_embedding[0, token, d]` Yes, that's what I have. Let me be careful about the `num_patches` variable. In the kernel, `num_patches` should probably be `seq_len` (197), since the output has that many tokens. Or I can pass `seq_len` as the total length. Let me clean up the variables. Actually, in the reference, `num_patches` is the number of image patches (196). Here's my C++ code structure: ```cpp at::Tensor forward_cuda( at::Tensor img, at::Tensor patch_weight, at::Tensor patch_bias, at::Tensor cls_token, at::Tensor pos_embedding, float emb_dropout_p, bool training, // Transformer parameters std::vector<at::Tensor> norm1_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> norm1_biases, std::vector<at::Tensor> qkv_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> qkv_biases, std::vector<at::Tensor> out_proj_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> out_proj_biases, std::vector<at::Tensor> norm2_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> norm2_biases, std::vector<at::Tensor> ffn1_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> ffn1_biases, std::vector<at::Tensor> ffn2_weights, std::vector<at::Tensor> ffn2_biases, // MLP head parameters at::Tensor mlp_w1, at::Tensor mlp_b1, at::Tensor mlp_w2, at::Tensor mlp_b2, // Config int patch_size, int depth, int heads, float dropout_p) { // 1. Preprocess auto x = preprocess_kernel_launch(img, patch_weight, patch_bias, cls_token, pos_embedding, patch_size, emb_dropout_p); // 2. Transformer for (int i = 0; i < depth; i++) { // Layer norm 1 auto x_norm = at::native_layer_norm(x, norm1_weights[i].sizes()[0], norm1_weights[i], norm1_biases[i], 1e-5)[0]; // Attention auto qkv = at::matmul(x_norm, qkv_weights[i].t()) + qkv_biases[i]; auto qkv_split = qkv.chunk(3, -1); auto q = qkv_split[0]; auto k = qkv_split[1]; auto v = qkv_split[2]; // Reshape int B = q.size(0); int S = q.size(1); int D = q.size(2) / heads; // head_dim q = q.view({B, S, heads, D}).transpose(1, 2); k = k.view({B, S, heads, D}).transpose(1, 2); v = v.view({B, S, heads, D}).transpose(1, 2); auto attn = at::_scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v); auto attn_out = std::get<0>(attn).transpose(1, 2).reshape({B, S, q.size(2) * heads}); attn_out = at::matmul(attn_out, out_proj_weights[i].t()) + out_proj_biases[i]; x = x + attn_out; // Layer norm 2 x_norm = at::native_layer_norm(x, norm2_weights[i].sizes()[0], norm2_weights[i], norm2_biases[i], 1e-5)[0]; // FFN auto h = at::matmul(x_norm, ffn1_weights[i].t()) + ffn1_biases[i]; h = at::gelu(h); h = at::matmul(h, ffn2_weights[i].t()) + ffn2_biases[i]; x = x + h; } // 3. Extract CLS auto cls = x.slice(1, 0, 1).squeeze(1); // 4. MLP head auto out = at::matmul(cls, mlp_w1.t()) + mlp_b1; out = at::gelu(out); out = at::matmul(out, mlp_w2.t()) + mlp_b2; return out; } ``` Wait, this has issues: 1. `at::native_layer_norm` returns a tuple. Let me write a kernel for the MLP head too. **MLP head kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void mlp_head_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, // [B, dim] const float* __restrict__ w1, // [mlp_dim, dim] const float* __restrict__ b1, // [mlp_dim] const float* __restrict__ w2, // [num_classes, mlp_dim] const float* __restrict__ b2, // [num_classes] float* __restrict__ out, // [B, num_classes] int B, int dim, int mlp_dim, int num_classes) { extern __shared__ float s_shared[]; // Layout: s_input[dim], s_hidden[mlp_dim] float* s_input = s_shared; float* s_hidden = s_shared + dim; int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load input for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { s_input[d] = x[b * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute first linear + GELU for (int m = tid; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; const float* w_row = w1 + m * dim; #pragma unroll 8 for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += s_input[d] * w_row[d]; } // GELU float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (sum + 0.044715f * sum * sum * sum))); s_hidden[m] = sum * cdf; } __syncthreads(); // Compute second linear for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; const float* w_row = w2 + n * mlp_dim; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w_row[m]; } out[b * num_classes + n] = sum; } } ``` This kernel is nice and simple. Using shared memory for the small intermediate activations is efficient. GELU approximation: `tanh` approximation is standard and matches PyTorch's `nn.GELU(approximate='tanh')`? Wait, PyTorch's `nn.GELU()` uses the exact integral by default, not the tanh approximation. Actually, `torch.nn.GELU` without the `approximate` parameter uses the exact Gaussian Error Linear Unit: `gelu(x) = x * Φ(x)` where `Φ(x)` is the CDF of standard normal. But for CUDA, the exact GELU is: `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` Computing `erf` can be expensive. PyTorch's `nn.GELU` uses a fast approximation internally for CUDA. For correctness matching, I should check if PyTorch uses an approximation or the exact formula. In recent PyTorch versions, the default is exact GELU (erf-based). But evaluating `erf` in CUDA requires special functions. I can use `erff` from CUDA math library. Or if speed is critical and the evaluator's PyTorch uses tanh approximation, I could use that. Actually, for the H100, `erf` is a hardware instruction or very fast intrinsic. To be safe and correct, I'll use the exact GELU formula: ```cuda float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.707106781f)); } ``` Wait, does `erff` give the same result as PyTorch? PyTorch uses a certain implementation of erf. CUDA's `erff` might differ in the last bit. For fp32 tolerance of 1e-4, this might be OK. But to be safe, I should check or use the tanh approximation if PyTorch does. Actually, looking at PyTorch source for GELU CUDA kernel: ```cpp template <typename T> __device__ __forceinline__ T GeluCU(const T x) { const T sqrt_param = T(M_SQRT1_2); const T cdf = normcdf(x); return x * cdf; } ``` And `normcdf` might use `erf` or an approximation. In CUDA, PyTorch likely uses `normcdff` or `erff`. Let me check... In recent PyTorch, the GELU implementation in ATen uses the exact `erf` formula. Actually, wait. Let me draft the complete Python file. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ cls_token, const float* __restrict__ pos_embedding, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int H, int W, int patch_size, int num_patches, int patch_dim, int dim, float dropout_p, unsigned int seed) { extern __shared__ float s_patch[]; int b = blockIdx.x; int token = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || token >= (num_patches + 1)) return; if (token == 0) { for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { out[(b * (num_patches + 1) + token) * dim + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; } return; } int patch_idx = token - 1; int patches_per_row = W / patch_size; int py = patch_idx / patches_per_row; int px = patch_idx % patches_per_row; int total_pixels = patch_dim; for (int i = tid; i < total_pixels; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (patch_size * patch_size); int rem = i % (patch_size * patch_size); int dy = rem / patch_size; int dx = rem % patch_size; int iy = py * patch_size + dy; int ix = px * patch_size + dx; s_patch[i] = img[((b * C + c) * H + iy) * W + ix]; } __syncthreads(); for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[d]; const float* w_row = weight + d * patch_dim; #pragma unroll 8 for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) { sum += s_patch[i] * w_row[i]; } sum += pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; if (dropout_p > 0.0f) { unsigned int rng = seed + b * (num_patches + 1) * dim + token * dim + d; rng = (rng * 1103515245u + 12345u); float rand_val = (float)(rng & 0x7fffffffu) / (float)0x7fffffffu; if (rand_val < dropout_p) { sum = 0.0f; } else { sum /= (1.0f - dropout_p); } } out[(b * (num_patches + 1) + token) * dim + d] = sum; } } __global__ void mlp_head_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w1, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2, const float* __restrict__ b2, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim, int mlp_dim, int num_classes) { extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_hidden = s_mem + dim; int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { s_input[d] = x[b * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); for (int m = tid; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; const float* w_row = w1 + m * dim; for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += s_input[d] * w_row[d]; } float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(sum * 0.7071067811865475f)); s_hidden[m] = sum * cdf; } __syncthreads(); for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; const float* w_row = w2 + n * mlp_dim; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w_row[m]; } out[b * num_classes + n] = sum; } } ''' cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor preprocess_cuda( torch::Tensor img, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor cls_token, torch::Tensor pos_embedding, int patch_size, float dropout_p) { int B = img.size(0); int C = img.size(1); int H = img.size(2); int W = img.size(3); int num_patches = (H / patch_size) * (W / patch_size); int seq_len = num_patches + 1; int dim = weight.size(0); int patch_dim = weight.size(1); auto out = torch::empty({B, seq_len, dim}, img.options()); dim3 grid(B, seq_len); dim3 block(256); size_t shared_mem = patch_dim * sizeof(float); unsigned int seed = 1234u; auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); preprocess_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem, stream>>>( img.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), cls_token.data_ptr<float>(), pos_embedding.contiguous().data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W, patch_size, num_patches, patch_dim, dim, dropout_p, seed ); return out; } torch::Tensor mlp_head_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2) { int B = x.size(0); int dim = x.size(1); int mlp_dim = w1.size(0); int num_classes = w2.size(0); auto out = torch::empty({B, num_classes}, x.options()); dim3 grid(B); dim3 block(256); size_t shared_mem = (dim + mlp_dim) * sizeof(float); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); mlp_head_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem, stream>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w1.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(), w2.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, dim, mlp_dim, num_classes ); return out; } ''' custom_ops = load_inline( name='custom_vit', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['preprocess_cuda', 'mlp_head_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() assert image_size % patch_size == 0 num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2 patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2 self.patch_size = patch_size self.num_patches = num_patches self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim)) self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim) self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim)) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout) self.dim = dim self.heads = heads self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder( nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout, batch_first=True), num_layers=depth ) self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity() self.mlp_head = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(dim, mlp_dim), nn.GELU(), nn.Dropout(dropout), nn.Linear(mlp_dim, num_classes) ) def forward(self, img): p = self.patch_size B = img.shape[0] # Fused preprocessing x = custom_ops.preprocess_cuda( img, self.patch_to_embedding.weight, self.patch_to_embedding.bias, self.cls_token.view(-1, self.dim), self.pos_embedding.view(-1, self.dim), p, self.dropout.p if self.training else 0.0 ) x = self.dropout(x) # Actually preprocess_cuda already applied dropout... For the transformer manual layers: ```python for layer in self.transformer.layers: x2 = F.layer_norm(x, layer.norm1.normalized_shape, layer.norm1.weight, layer.norm1.bias, layer.norm1.eps) # Attention if hasattr(layer.self_attn, 'in_proj_weight') and layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight is not None: qkv = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) else: q = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.q_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[:self.dim]) k = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.k_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[self.dim:2*self.dim]) v = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.v_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[2*self.dim:]) q = q.view(B, -1, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(B, -1, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(B, -1, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) attn = F.scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v) attn = attn.transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, -1, self.dim) x2 = F.linear(attn, layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) x = x + layer.dropout1(x2) x2 = F.layer_norm(x, layer.norm2.normalized_shape, layer.norm2.weight, layer.norm2.bias, layer.norm2.eps) # FFN if hasattr(layer, 'activation'): activation_fn = layer.activation else: activation_fn = F.relu x2 = F.linear(x2, layer.linear1.weight, layer.linear1.bias) x2 = activation_fn(x2) x2 = F.linear(layer.dropout(x2), layer.linear2.weight, layer.linear2.bias) x = x + layer.dropout2(x2) ``` Wait, `layer.activation` might be a module. Now, let me draft the attention mechanism more carefully: ```python # x2 is [B, S, D] after layer norm B, S, _ = x2.shape # QKV projection if hasattr(layer.self_attn, 'in_proj_weight') and layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight is not None: qkv = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.in_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias) q, k, v = qkv.chunk(3, dim=-1) else: q = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.q_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[:self.dim]) k = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.k_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[self.dim:2*self.dim]) v = F.linear(x2, layer.self_attn.v_proj_weight, layer.self_attn.in_proj_bias[2*self.dim:]) # Reshape for attention q = q.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) # Attention attn = F.scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v) attn = attn.transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, S, self.dim) # Output projection x2 = F.linear(attn, layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) # Residual + dropout x = x + layer.dropout1(x2) ``` Wait, does `F.scaled_dot_product_attention` apply dropout? The cls_token is expanded (.broadcasted) across batch dimension. In my kernel, I directly use `cls_token` for each batch item. Are these equivalent? Yes, because `expand` doesn't copy memory, and concatenation with `cat` does copy. For dropout=0, the mathematical result is the same. Actually, I should also think about performance of the custom kernel. For batch=2, seq=197, each CUDA block handles one token. So I launch 394 blocks total. Each block has 256 threads. Block 0 handles CLS for batch 0. Block 1 handles patch 0 for batch 0. Etc. For the CLS token block, only 512 threads need to do work, but we have 256 threads, so each does 2 elements. That's fine. For image patch blocks, the 256 threads first load patch pixels into shared memory. There are 768 pixels, so each thread loads 3 pixels. Then each thread computes 2 output elements (since dim=512 and 256 threads handle 512 output dims with 2 computations each). The loop `for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x)` handles this. Wait, `blockDim.x` is 256, `dim` is 512. So each thread computes 2 output elements. The `for` loop approach with `d += blockDim.x` is a common pattern. Actually, since 512 is exactly 2 * 256, each thread would do exactly 2 iterations. But wait - tid ranges from 0 to 255. First iteration: d = tid (0 to 255). Second iteration: d = tid + 256 (256 to 511). Yes, 2 iterations per thread. Good. But inside the loop, we do a dot product of 768 elements. So total work per thread: 2 * 768 = 1536 multiplications + additions. This should be fast. One optimization: the weight matrix `weight[dim, patch_dim]` is accessed as `weight[d * patch_dim + i]`. For consecutive threads in a warp accessing `weight[d * patch_dim + i]` where d differs by 1, the access is strided by `patch_dim=768` floats = 3072 bytes. This means consecutive threads in a warp access memory with a stride of 3072 bytes. On modern GPUs, strided access is not fully coalesced, but the L1 cache and global memory coalescing should still handle it reasonably well because the stride corresponds to about 48 cache lines (64 bytes each). Actually, 3072 bytes is a large stride, so threads in a warp access memory quite far apart. This is NOT coalesced. For better coalescing, I could transpose the weight matrix and access it as `weight[i * dim + d]`. Then consecutive threads (with consecutive `d`) would access consecutive memory: `weight[i * dim + tid]`, `weight[i * dim + tid + 1]`, etc. This is perfectly coalesced! But `self.patch_to_embedding.weight` is stored as [dim=512, patch_dim=768] in PyTorch's nn.Linear. So `weight[d, i]` is the original layout. If I transpose it in Python (`.t()`) and pass the transposed weight, then in the kernel I can access it as `weight[i * dim + d]` which is coalesced. Yes! Let me pass `self.patch_to_embedding.weight.t()` instead of `.weight`. And adjust the kernel indexing. In the kernel: ```cuda const float* w_col = weight + i * dim; // weight is now [patch_dim, dim] for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[d]; for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) { sum += s_patch[i] * w_col[d]; } } ``` Wait, but if weight is [patch_dim, dim], then for a fixed i, `weight[i * dim + d]` is contiguous in d. But in my sum_i loop, for each i, I access `weight[i * dim + d]` where `d` is the thread id. This is perfectly coalesced across threads in a warp! However, I have the sum loop inside. So for each i, the threads in the block access `weight[i * dim + tid]`. Yes, coalesced. Let me restructure: ```cuda for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[d]; for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) { sum += s_patch[i] * weight[i * dim + d]; } out[...] = sum + pos_embedding[...]; } ``` This is much better for memory coalescing. For the MLP head kernel, similar optimization applies. Currently I pass w1 as [mlp_dim, dim]. Accessing `w1[m * dim + d]` has d varying within a warp's threads... wait, in my MLP head kernel: ```cuda for (int m = tid; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; const float* w_row = w1 + m * dim; for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += s_input[d] * w_row[d]; } } ``` Here m varies across threads. Accessing `w_row[d]` for the SAME d but DIFFERENT m across threads in a warp. This is strided by `dim` floats = 2048 bytes. Not fully coalesced. If I transpose w1 to [dim, mlp_dim] and restructure: ```cuda for (int m = tid; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += s_input[d] * w1[d * mlp_dim + m]; } } ``` Now for a fixed d, threads access `w1[d * mlp_dim + m]` where m varies by thread. This is perfectly coalesced! Great, let me apply this transposition optimization. For w2 in MLP head: w2 is [num_classes, mlp_dim] = [10, 2048]. Current: ```cuda for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; const float* w_row = w2 + n * mlp_dim; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w_row[m]; } } ``` Here n varies across threads. For fixed m, threads access `w_row[m]` which is `w2[n * mlp_dim + m]`. Stride is mlp_dim=2048 floats = 8192 bytes. Not coalesced. Transpose to [mlp_dim, num_classes]: ```cuda for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w2[m * num_classes + n]; } } ``` Now for fixed m, threads access `w2[m * num_classes + n]`. Since n is tid, this is perfectly coalesced (consecutive floats)! Excellent. Let me transpose all weights in Python before passing to CUDA. One important detail: the weights from `nn.Linear` are stored as [out_features, in_features]. For `patch_to_embedding`, weight is [dim, patch_dim]. Transpose gives [patch_dim, dim]. I'll pass transposed weights to the CUDA functions. Actually, I need to be careful: `weight.t()` creates a view with a transpose, which might not be contiguous. I should call `.t().contiguous()` to ensure the underlying memory is in the transposed order, so the coalescing actually happens. OR, I could rely on `.t()` returning a strided tensor, and the CUDA kernel accesses according to the strides. But I don't pass strides to the kernel currently; I assume row-major contiguous layout. So yes, I'll use `weight.t().contiguous()`. Now, for the bias terms, they are 1D and accessed by `bias[d]` or `b1[m]`. Access patterns are fine since each thread reads its own bias element. OK, let me now write the final code. One more important check: does `nn.Linear` store weight as [out_features, in_features]? Yes. So `self.patch_to_embedding.weight` is [512, 768]. Good. Now let me write the actual kernel code carefully. Actually, for the preprocessing kernel, I should use patch_dim=768, dim=512. In the kernel: ```cuda extern __shared__ float s_patch[]; int b = blockIdx.x; int token = blockIdx.y; // token 0 = CLS, tokens 1..num_patches = image patches if (token == 0) { for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float val = cls_token[d] + pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; out[(b * seq_len + token) * dim + d] = val; } return; } // token > 0 int patch_idx = token - 1; int patches_per_row = W / patch_size; int py = patch_idx / patches_per_row; int px = patch_idx % patches_per_row; // Load pixels for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < patch_dim; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (patch_size * patch_size); int rem = i % (patch_size * patch_size); int dy = rem / patch_size; int dx = rem % patch_size; int iy = py * patch_size + dy; int ix = px * patch_size + dx; s_patch[i] = img[((b * C + c) * H + iy) * W + ix]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute linear projection with transposed weight [patch_dim, dim] for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { float sum = bias[d]; for (int i = 0; i < patch_dim; i++) { sum += s_patch[i] * weight[i * dim + d]; } sum += pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; out[(b * seq_len + token) * dim + d] = sum; } ``` This looks correct. Now let me write the MLP head kernel similarly. ```cuda __global__ void mlp_head_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w1, // [dim, mlp_dim] transposed const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2, // [mlp_dim, num_classes] transposed const float* __restrict__ b2, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim, int mlp_dim, int num_classes) { extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_hidden = s_mem + dim; // Actually, need to handle shared memory carefully // Wait, the shared memory size is passed at launch as (dim + mlp_dim) * sizeof(float) // But if dim=512 and mlp_dim=2048, total is 2556 floats = ~10KB. Fine. int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load input [dim] for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { s_input[d] = x[b * dim + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute hidden [mlp_dim] = input @ w1 + b1, then GELU for (int m = tid; m < mlp_dim; m += blockDim.x) { float sum = b1[m]; for (int d = 0; d < dim; d++) { sum += s_input[d] * w1[d * mlp_dim + m]; } float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(sum * 0.7071067811865475f)); s_hidden[m] = sum * cdf; } __syncthreads(); // Compute output [num_classes] = hidden @ w2 + b2 for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w2[m * num_classes + n]; } out[b * num_classes + n] = sum; } } ``` Wait, `erff` might not be available or might behave differently from PyTorch's GELU. Let me draft it: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ img, const float* __restrict__ weight, // [patch_dim, dim] transposed const float* __restrict__ bias, // [dim] const float* __restrict__ cls_token, // [dim] const float* __restrict__ pos_embedding, // [seq_len, dim] float* __restrict__ out, // [B, seq_len, dim] int B, int C, int H, int W, int patch_size, int num_patches, int patch_dim, int dim) { extern __shared__ float s_patch[]; int b = blockIdx.x; int token = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || token >= (num_patches + 1)) return; if (token == 0) { for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { out[(b * (num_patches + 1) + token) * dim + d] = cls_token[d] + pos_embedding[token * dim + d]; } return; } int patch_idx = token - 1; int patches_per_row = W / patch_size; int py = patch_idx / patches_per_row; int px = patch_idx % patches_per_row; for (int i = tid; i < patch_dim; i += blockDim.x) { int c = i / (patch_size * patch_size); int rem = i % (patch_size * patch_size); int dy = rem / patch_size; int dx = rem % patch_size; int iy = py * patch_size + dy; int ix = px * patch_size + dx; s_patch[i] = img[((b * C + c) * H + iy) * W + ix]; } __syncthreads(); for (int d = tid; d < dim; dim + d] = sum; } } __global__ void mlp_head_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, // [B, dim] const float* __restrict__ w1, // [dim, mlp_dim] transposed const float* __restrict__ b1, // [mlp_dim] const float* __restrict__ w2, // [mlp_dim, num_classes] transposed const float* __restrict__ b2, // [num_classes] float* __restrict__ out, // [B, num_classes] int B, int dim, int mlp_dim, int num_classes) { extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_hidden = s_mem + dim; int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int d = tid; d < dim; d += blockDim.x) { cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(sum * 0.7071067811865475f)); s_hidden[m] = sum * cdf; } __syncthreads(); for (int n = tid; n < num_classes; n += blockDim.x) { float sum = b2[n]; for (int m = 0; m < mlp_dim; m++) { sum += s_hidden[m] * w2[m * num_classes + n]; } out[b * num_classes + n] = sum; } } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor preprocess_cuda( torch::Tensor img, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor cls_token, torch::Tensor pos_embedding, int patch_size) { int B = img.size(0); int C = img.size(1); int H = img.size(2); int W = img.size(3); int num_patches = (H / patch_size) * (W / patch_size); int seq_len = num_patches + 1; int dim = weight.size(1); int patch_dim = weight.size(0); auto out = torch::empty({B, seq_len, dim}, img.options()); dim3 grid(B, seq_len); dim3 block(256); size_t shared_mem = patch_dim * sizeof(float); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); preprocess_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem, stream>>>( img.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), cls_token.data_ptr<float>(), pos_embedding.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W, patch_size, num_patches, patch_dim, dim ); return out; } torch::Tensor mlp_head_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2) { int B = x.size(0); int dim = w2.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, dim, mlp_dim, num_classes ); return out; } ''' custom_ops = load_inline( name='custom_vit_ops', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['preprocess_cuda', 'mlp_head_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], extra_cflags=['-O3'], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, image_size, patch_size, num_classes, dim, depth, heads, mlp_dim, channels=3, dropout=0.1, emb_dropout=0.1): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() assert image_size % patch_size == 0, "Image dimensions must be divisible by the patch size." num_patches = (image_size // patch_size) ** 2 patch_dim = channels * patch_size ** 2 self.patch_size = patch_size self.pos_embedding = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, num_patches + 1, dim)) self.patch_to_embedding = nn.Linear(patch_dim, dim) self.cls_token = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, 1, dim)) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(emb_dropout) self.dim = dim self.heads = heads self.transformer = nn.TransformerEncoder( nn.TransformerEncoderLayer(d_model=dim, nhead=heads, dim_feedforward=mlp_dim, dropout=dropout, batch_first=True), num_layers=depth ) self.to_cls_token = nn.Identity() self.pos_embedding.view(-1, self.pos_embedding.size(2)).contiguous(), p ) x = self.dropout(x) # Custom manual transformer to ensure efficient attention path B = x.size(0) S = x.size(1) for layer in self.heads).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(B, S, self.heads, self.dim // self.heads).transpose(1, 2) attn = layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) x = x + layer.dropout1(attn) # Norm 2 + FFN sublayer x_norm = F.layer_norm(x, layer.norm2.normalized_shape, layer.norm2.weight, layer.norm2.bias, layer.norm2.eps) ffn = F.linear(x_norm, layer.linear1.weight, layer.linear1.bias) ffn = layer.activation(ffn) ffn = F.linear(layer.dropout(ffn), layer.linear2.weight, layer.linear2.bias) x = x + layer.dropout2(ffn) x = x[:, 0] x = self.to_cls_token(x) # Fused MLP head when dropout is not active dropout_module = self.mlp_head[2] if not self.training or dropout_module.p == 0.0: x = custom_ops.mlp_head_cuda( x.contiguous(), self.mlp_head[0].weight.t().contiguous(), self.mlp_head[0].bias.contiguous(), self.mlp_head[3].weight.t().contiguous(), self.mlp_head[3].bias.contiguous() ) else: x = self.mlp_head(x) return x ``` Also, for the attention output: ```python attn = F.linear(attn, layer.self_attn.out_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.out_proj.bias) x.add_(layer.dropout1(attn)) ``` For the FFN: ```python ffn = F.linear(layer.dropout(ffn), layer.linear2.weight, layer.linear2.bias) x.add_(layer.dropout2(ffn)) ``` But wait, `x.add_(y)` modifies `x` in-place. Is this safe? Yes, because we don't need the old `x` after the add. The only issue is if `x` is needed later in the same computation graph for gradient computation... but we're only doing forward here. And even for backward, PyTorch's autograd handles in-place operations correctly if they're valid. Actually, `x.add_(y)` creates an in-place addition. For a module's forward method, if we don't need `x` afterwards, it's fine. The gradient computation would also work. But in PyTorch, in-place operations can sometimes cause issues with autograd if the tensor is needed for other computations. But in the transformer residual connection, `x` is only used for the add and then discarded. So `x.add_(attn)` is completely safe. This saves 2 allocations per layer = 12 allocations for 6 layers. Each allocation for [2, 197, 512] is about 800KB. Saving ~9MB of allocations might save a few microseconds. Also, `x_norm = F.layer_norm(x, ...)` creates a new tensor. This is unavoidable because we need the normalized version for the sublayer while preserving `x` for the residual. Wait, can we fuse layer_norm + residual_add? PyTorch doesn't have a fused op. But conceptually: `x_new = x + f(layer_norm(x))`. We need `layer_norm(x)` as input to `f`, and then we add it to `x`. So we can't avoid creating the `layer_norm(x)` tensor. But for the residual add, `x.add_(attn)` modifies `x` in-place. This saves one tensor allocation. For attention: `qkv = F.linear(x_norm, ...)` creates a new tensor. Can we avoid this? Only by writing a custom kernel that computes QKV and immediately does attention without creating the full QKV tensor. That's what fused attention kernels do, but it's complex. For now, in-place adds are a cheap win. Let me also check: does `F.layer_norm` have an in-place version? No, LayerNorm doesn't have an in-place variant because the mean/variance computation requires reading the original values, and writing back in-place is fine for output values but the original values are needed for statistics. Actually, for LayerNorm, we CAN write in-place because the output overwrites the input. PyTorch's `layer_norm` doesn't have an in-place variant, but `F.layer_norm` returns a new tensor. I'll stick with `F.layer_norm`. For `view` and `transpose`, these don't allocate new memory. Good. One more optimization: after `x = x[:, 0].contiguous()`, the result is contiguous. Good. Alright, let me write the final code and compile it. Actually, rather than compile and test iteratively, let me try to get it right on the first try. Wait, that's risky. I should compile first to check for syntax errors, then run correctness. Let me write the code. But first, one more consideration: `load_inline` might take a while to compile. I should keep the code relatively short. Here's the final code I plan to submit for compilation: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = reasoning
The compile failed because the CUDA source and C++ source were not merged correctly. The `load_inline` function expects both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`, but the CUDA calls in C++ need the CUDA headers. Let me put everything in `cuda_sources` or use a different approach. Actually, looking at the error more carefully: the C++ code includes `dim3`, `<<<>>>`, and calls to `preprocess_kernel` which are CUDA-specific. These should be in a `.cu` file. The `cpp_sources` is compiled with just the C++ compiler (g++), not nvcc. The proper way to do this is to put all host-side code (the `torch::Tensor` wrappers) in `cuda_sources` as well, or use the `with_cuda=True` flag. Actually, looking at PyTorch docs, `load_inline` expects that: - `cpp_sources`: C++ host code - `cuda_sources`: CUDA device and host code When using `cuda_sources`, the cuda code is compiled by nvcc and the cpp code by gcc. But the cpp code shouldn't contain `dim3` or kernel launches. So I need to move all the host-side wrapper code into `cuda_sources` as well. Let me rewrite properly by putting everything into `cuda_sources` and keeping `cpp_sources` empty or minimal.
